</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N_{C}={\frac {\lambda _{C}}{\lambda }}N_{A0}\left(1-e^{-\lambda t}\right).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="N_{C}={\frac {\lambda _{C}}{\lambda }}N_{A0}\left(1-e^{-\lambda t}\right)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6d99cc749c93f1c24ef2618d86e85608b859a1fc" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:25.971ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>because the fraction <span class="texhtml"><i>λ<sub>B</sub></i>/<i>λ</i></span> of nuclei decay into <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i></span> while the fraction <span class="texhtml"><i>λ<sub>C</sub></i>/<i>λ</i></span> of nuclei decay into <span class="texhtml"><i>C</i></span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corollaries_of_laws">Corollaries of laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Corollaries of laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The above equations can also be written using quantities related to the number of nuclide particles <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i></span> in a sample;
</p>
<ul><li>The activity: <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i> = <i>λN</i></span>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a>: <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i> = <i>N</i>/<i>N</i><sub>A</sub></span>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>: <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i> = <i>Mn</i> = <i>MN</i>/<i>N</i><sub>A</sub></span>.</li></ul>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i><sub>A</sub></span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7023602214076000000♠"></span>6.022<span style="margin-left:.25em;">140</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">76</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup> mol<sup>−1</sup></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physconst-NA_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-physconst-NA-39">[37]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro constant</a>, <span class="texhtml"><i>M</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Molar_mass" title="Molar mass">molar mass</a> of the substance in kg/mol, and the amount of the substance <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> is in <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">moles</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decay_timing:_definitions_and_relations">Decay timing: definitions and relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Decay timing: definitions and relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_constant_and_mean-life">Time constant and mean-life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Time constant and mean-life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For the one-decay solution <i><span class="texhtml">A → B</span></i>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle N=N_{0}\,e^{-{\lambda }t}=N_{0}\,e^{-t/\tau },\,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="N=N_{0}\,e^{-{\lambda }t}=N_{0}\,e^{-t/\tau },\,\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/26927776582294591c8d1d4460fb7d035cd183f9" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:24.916ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>the equation indicates that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_constant" title="Decay constant">decay constant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>λ</i></span> has units of <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i><sup>−1</sup></span>, and can thus also be represented as 1/<span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i></span>, where <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i></span> is a characteristic time of the process called the <i><a href="/wiki/Time_constant" title="Time constant">time constant</a></i>.
</p><p>In a radioactive decay process, this time constant is also the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> for decaying atoms. Each atom "lives" for a finite amount of time before it decays, and it may be shown that this mean lifetime is the <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_mean" title="Arithmetic mean">arithmetic mean</a> of all the atoms' lifetimes, and that it is <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i></span>, which again is related to the decay constant as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau ={\frac {1}{\lambda }}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>.</mo>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Half-life">Half-life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Half-life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A more commonly used parameter is the <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i><sub>1/2</sub></span>. Given a sample of a particular radionuclide, the half-life is the time taken for half the radionuclide's atoms to decay. For the case of one-decay nuclear reactions:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle N=N_{0}\,e^{-{\lambda }t}=N_{0}\,e^{-t/\tau },\,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>N</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle N=N_{0}\,e^{-{\lambda }t}=N_{0}\,e^{-t/\tau },\,\!}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/26927776582294591c8d1d4460fb7d035cd183f9" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:24.916ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>the half-life is related to the decay constant as follows: set <span class="texhtml">N = <i>N</i><sub>0</sub>/2</span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i></span> = <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i><sub>1/2</sub></span> to obtain
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle t_{1/2}={\frac {\ln 2}{\lambda }}=\tau \ln 2.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi>t</mi>

<mn>1</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>τ<!-- τ --></mi>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mn>2.</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t_{1/2}={\frac {\ln 2}{\lambda }}=\tau \ln 2.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle t_{1/2}={\frac {\ln 2}{\lambda }}=\tau \ln 2.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/937374af2b4db7f946bd223a44c2559a8c034e94" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:19.785ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>This relationship between the half-life and the decay constant shows that highly radioactive substances are quickly spent, while those that radiate weakly endure longer. <a href="/wiki/List_of_radioactive_nuclides_by_half-life" title="List of radioactive nuclides by half-life">Half-lives of known radionuclides</a> vary by almost 54 orders of magnitude, from more than <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7024225000000000000♠"></span>2.25(9)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>24</sup></span> years (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7031690000000000000♠"></span>6.9<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>31</sup></span> sec) for the very nearly stable nuclide <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_tellurium" title="Isotopes of tellurium"><sup>128</sup>Te</a>, to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6977859999999999999♠"></span>8.6(6)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−23</sup></span> seconds for the highly unstable nuclide <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen"><sup>5</sup>H</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The factor of <span class="texhtml">ln(2)</span> in the above relations results from the fact that the concept of "half-life" is merely a way of selecting a different base other than the natural base <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span> for the lifetime expression. The time constant <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i></span> is the <span style="white-space: nowrap;"><span style="font-style: italic;"><i>e</i></span><span style="font-size: 40%;"> </span><span style="font-size: 70%;"><span style="vertical-align: 0.7em;">−1</span><span style="font-size: 40%;"> </span></span></span>-life, the time until only 1/<i>e</i> remains, about 36.8%, rather than the 50% in the half-life of a radionuclide. Thus, <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i></span> is longer than <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub></span>. The following equation can be shown to be valid:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle N(t)=N_{0}\,e^{-t/\tau }=N_{0}\,2^{-t/t_{1/2}}.\,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>N</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>τ<!-- τ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>N</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</msub>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N(t)=N_{0}\,e^{-t/\tau }=N_{0}\,2^{-t/t_{1/2}}.\,\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle N(t)=N_{0}\,e^{-t/\tau }=N_{0}\,2^{-t/t_{1/2}}.\,\!}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e6102327b6aa6ef4aad8555050b6afefcc1dd823" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:30.267ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Since radioactive decay is exponential with a constant probability, each process could as easily be described with a different constant time period that (for example) gave its "(1/3)-life" (how long until only 1/3 is left) or "(1/10)-life" (a time period until only 10% is left), and so on. Thus, the choice of <span class="texhtml"><i>τ</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i><sub>1/2</sub></span> for marker-times, are only for convenience, and from convention. They reflect a fundamental principle only in so much as they show that the <i>same proportion</i> of a given radioactive substance will decay, during any time-period that one chooses.
</p><p>Mathematically, the <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i><sup>th</sup></span> life for the above situation would be found in the same way as above—by setting <i><span class="texhtml">N = N<sub>0</sub>/n</span></i>, <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i> = <i>T</i><sub>1/<i>n</i></sub></span> and substituting into the decay solution to obtain
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle t_{1/n}={\frac {\ln n}{\lambda }}=\tau \ln n.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>τ<!-- τ --></mi>
<mi>ln</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t_{1/n}={\frac {\ln n}{\lambda }}=\tau \ln n.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle t_{1/n}={\frac {\ln n}{\lambda }}=\tau \ln n.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/adedff1071f83854a0f6fddfcde6af18ae384f83" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:20.413ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_for_carbon-14">Example for carbon-14</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Example for carbon-14">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">Carbon-14</a> has a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003570000000000000♠"></span>5700(30)</span> years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> and a decay rate of 14 disintegrations per minute (dpm) per gram of natural carbon.
</p><p>If an artifact is found to have radioactivity of 4 dpm per gram of its present C, we can find the approximate age of the object using the above equation:
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Changing_rates">Changing rates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Changing rates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The radioactive decay modes of <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internal_conversion" title="Internal conversion">internal conversion</a> are known to be slightly sensitive to chemical and environmental effects that change the electronic structure of the atom, which in turn affects the presence of <b>1s</b> and <b>2s</b> electrons that participate in the decay process. A small number of nuclides are affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_bonds" title="Chemical bonds">chemical bonds</a> can affect the rate of electron capture to a small degree (in general, less than 1%) depending on the proximity of electrons to the nucleus. In <sup>7</sup>Be, a difference of 0.9% has been observed between half-lives in metallic and insulating environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WangYan2006_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-WangYan2006-41">[39]</a></sup> This relatively large effect is because beryllium is a small atom whose valence electrons are in <b>2s</b> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbitals</a>, which are subject to electron capture in <sup>7</sup>Be because (like all <b>s</b> atomic orbitals in all atoms) they naturally penetrate into the nucleus.
</p><p>In 1992, Jung et al. of the Darmstadt Heavy-Ion Research group observed an accelerated β<sup>−</sup> decay of <sup>163</sup>Dy<sup>66+</sup>. Although neutral <sup>163</sup>Dy is a stable isotope, the fully ionized <sup>163</sup>Dy<sup>66+</sup> undergoes β<sup>−</sup> decay <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bound-state_beta_decay" title="Bound-state beta decay">into the K and L shells</a> to <sup>163</sup>Ho<sup>66+</sup> with a half-life of 47 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JungBosch1992_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-JungBosch1992-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhenium-187" title="Rhenium-187">Rhenium-187</a> is another spectacular example. <sup>187</sup>Re normally undergoes <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> to <sup>187</sup>Os with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 41.6 × 10<sup>9</sup> years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> but studies using fully ionised <sup>187</sup><a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">Re</a> atoms (bare nuclei) have found that this can decrease to only 32.9 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> This is attributed to "<a href="/wiki/Beta_decay#Bound-state_β-_decay" title="Beta decay">bound-state β<sup>−</sup> decay</a>" of the fully ionised atom – the electron is emitted into the "K-shell" (<b>1s</b> atomic orbital), which cannot occur for neutral atoms in which all low-lying bound states are occupied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bosch1996_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bosch1996-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DecayRate_vs_Solar_Time.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="342" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/DecayRate_vs_Solar_Time.png/290px-DecayRate_vs_Solar_Time.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d3/DecayRate_vs_Solar_Time.png 1.5x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DecayRate_vs_Solar_Time.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of diurnal and seasonal variations in gamma ray detector response.</div></div></div>
<p>A number of experiments have found that decay rates of other modes of artificial and naturally occurring radioisotopes are, to a high degree of precision, unaffected by external conditions such as temperature, pressure, the chemical environment, and electric, magnetic, or gravitational fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> Comparison of laboratory experiments over the last century, studies of the Oklo <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear reactor</a> (which exemplified the effects of thermal neutrons on nuclear decay), and astrophysical observations of the luminosity decays of distant supernovae (which occurred far away so the light has taken a great deal of time to reach us), for example, strongly indicate that unperturbed decay rates have been constant (at least to within the limitations of small experimental errors) as a function of time as well.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Entire paragraph needs sourcing (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Recent results suggest the possibility that decay rates might have a weak dependence on environmental factors. It has been suggested that measurements of decay rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon-32" title="Silicon-32">silicon-32</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manganese-54" title="Manganese-54">manganese-54</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radium-226" title="Radium-226">radium-226</a> exhibit small seasonal variations (of the order of 0.1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>  However, such measurements are highly susceptible to systematic errors, and a subsequent paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> has found no evidence for such correlations in seven other isotopes (<sup>22</sup>Na, <sup>44</sup>Ti, <sup>108</sup>Ag, <sup>121</sup>Sn, <sup>133</sup>Ba, <sup>241</sup>Am, <sup>238</sup>Pu), and sets upper limits on the size of any such effects. The decay of <a href="/wiki/Radon-222" title="Radon-222">radon-222</a> was once reported to exhibit large 4% peak-to-peak seasonal variations (see plot),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SturrockSteinitz2012_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-SturrockSteinitz2012-51">[49]</a></sup>  which were proposed to be related to either <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flare</a> activity or the distance from the Sun, but detailed analysis of the experiment's design flaws, along with comparisons to other, much more stringent and systematically controlled, experiments refute this claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="GSI_anomaly">GSI anomaly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: GSI anomaly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/GSI_anomaly" title="GSI anomaly">GSI anomaly</a></div>
<p>An unexpected series of experimental results for the rate of decay of heavy <a href="/wiki/Highly_charged_ion" title="Highly charged ion">highly charged</a> radioactive <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> circulating in a <a href="/wiki/Storage_ring" title="Storage ring">storage ring</a> has provoked theoretical activity in an effort to find a convincing explanation. The rates of <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak</a> decay of two radioactive species with half lives of about 40 s and 200 s are found to have a significant <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillatory</a> <a href="/wiki/Modulation" title="Modulation">modulation</a>, with a period of about 7 s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KienleBosch2013_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-KienleBosch2013-53">[51]</a></sup>
The observed phenomenon is known as the <a href="/wiki/GSI_anomaly" title="GSI anomaly">GSI anomaly</a>, as the storage ring is a facility at the <a href="/wiki/GSI_Helmholtz_Centre_for_Heavy_Ion_Research" title="GSI Helmholtz Centre for Heavy Ion Research">GSI Helmholtz Centre for Heavy Ion Research</a> in <a href="/wiki/Darmstadt" title="Darmstadt">Darmstadt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>.  As the decay process produces an <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrino</a>, some of the proposed explanations for the observed rate oscillation invoke neutrino properties. Initial ideas related to <a href="/wiki/Neutrino#Flavor_oscillations" title="Neutrino">flavour oscillation</a> met with skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giunti2009_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giunti2009-54">[52]</a></sup>  A more recent proposal involves mass differences between neutrino mass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eigenstates" title="Eigenstates">eigenstates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal2016_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gal2016-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theoretical_basis">Theoretical basis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Theoretical basis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> that constitute nuclei, as well as other particles that approach close enough to them, are governed by several interactions. The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a> (also known as <i>residual</i> <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong force</a>), not observed at the familiar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroscopic" title="Macroscopic">macroscopic</a> scale, is the most powerful force over subatomic distances. The <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">electrostatic force</a> is almost always significant, and, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak nuclear force</a> is also involved.
</p><p>The combined effects of these forces produces a number of different phenomena in which energy may be released by rearrangement of particles in the nucleus, or else the change of one type of particle into others. These rearrangements and transformations may be hindered energetically so that they do not occur immediately. In certain cases, random <a href="/wiki/Quantum_fluctuation" title="Quantum fluctuation">quantum vacuum fluctuations</a> are theorized to promote relaxation to a lower energy state (the "decay") in a phenomenon known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_tunneling" title="Quantum tunneling">quantum tunneling</a>. Radioactive decay <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of nuclides has been measured over timescales of 54 orders of magnitude, from <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6977859999999999999♠"></span>8.6(6)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−23</sup></span> seconds (for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-5" title="Hydrogen-5">hydrogen-5</a>) to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7031710000000000000♠"></span>7.10(28)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>31</sup></span> seconds (for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tellurium-128" title="Tellurium-128">tellurium-128</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> The limits of these timescales are set by the sensitivity of instrumentation only, and there are no known natural limits to how brief<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> or long a decay <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> for radioactive decay of a <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclide</a> may be.
</p><p>The decay process, like all hindered energy transformations, may be analogized by a snowfield on a mountain. While <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> between the ice crystals may be supporting the snow's weight, the system is inherently unstable with regard to a state of lower potential energy. A disturbance would thus facilitate the path to a state of greater <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>; the system will move towards the ground state, producing heat, and the total energy will be distributable over a larger number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_states" title="Quantum states">quantum states</a> thus resulting in an <a href="/wiki/Avalanche" title="Avalanche">avalanche</a>. The <i>total</i> energy does not change in this process, but, because of the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>, avalanches have only been observed in one direction and that is toward the "<a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a>" — the state with the largest number of ways in which the available energy could be distributed.
</p><p>Such a collapse (a gamma-ray <i>decay event</i>) requires a specific <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation energy</a>. For a snow avalanche, this energy comes as a disturbance from outside the system, although such disturbances can be arbitrarily small. In the case of an excited <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a> decaying by gamma radiation in a <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_emission" title="Spontaneous emission">spontaneous emission</a> of electromagnetic radiation, the arbitrarily small disturbance comes from <a href="/wiki/Quantum_fluctuation" title="Quantum fluctuation">quantum vacuum fluctuations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>A radioactive nucleus (or any excited system in quantum mechanics) is unstable, and can, thus, <i>spontaneously</i> stabilize to a less-excited system. The resulting transformation alters the structure of the nucleus and results in the emission of either a photon or a high-velocity particle that has mass (such as an electron, <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a>, or other type).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_applications">Occurrence and applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Occurrence and applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Bang_theory" title="Big Bang theory">Big Bang theory</a>, stable isotopes of the lightest three elements (<a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">He</a>, and traces of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">Li</a>) were produced very shortly after the emergence of the universe, in a process called <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>. These lightest stable nuclides (including <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>) survive to today, but any radioactive isotopes of the light elements produced in the Big Bang (such as <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>) have long since decayed. Isotopes of elements heavier than boron were not produced at all in the Big Bang, and these first five elements do not have any long-lived radioisotopes. Thus, all radioactive nuclei are, therefore, relatively young with respect to the birth of the universe, having formed later in various other types of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> (in particular, <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>), and also during ongoing interactions between stable isotopes and energetic particles. For example, <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">carbon-14</a>, a radioactive nuclide with a half-life of only <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003570000000000000♠"></span>5700(30)</span> years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> is constantly produced in Earth's upper atmosphere due to interactions between cosmic rays and nitrogen.
</p><p>Nuclides that are produced by radioactive decay are called <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic_nuclide" title="Radiogenic nuclide">radiogenic nuclides</a>, whether they themselves are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable</a> or not. There exist stable radiogenic nuclides that were formed from short-lived <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct radionuclides</a> in the early Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> The extra presence of these stable radiogenic nuclides (such as xenon-129 from extinct <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">iodine-129</a>) against the background of primordial <a href="/wiki/Stable_nuclide" title="Stable nuclide">stable nuclides</a> can be inferred by various means.
</p><p>Radioactive decay has been put to use in the technique of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotopic_labeling" title="Radioisotopic labeling">radioisotopic labeling</a>, which is used to track the passage of a chemical substance through a complex system (such as a living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a>). A sample of the substance is synthesized with a high concentration of unstable atoms. The presence of the substance in one or another part of the system is determined by detecting the locations of decay events.
</p><p>On the premise that radioactive decay is truly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Random" title="Random">random</a> (rather than merely <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaotic</a>), it has been used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardware_random-number_generator" title="Hardware random-number generator">hardware random-number generators</a>. Because the process is not thought to vary significantly in mechanism over time, it is also a valuable tool in estimating the absolute ages of certain materials. For geological materials, the radioisotopes and some of their decay products become trapped when a rock solidifies, and can then later be used (subject to many well-known qualifications) to estimate the date of the solidification. These include checking the results of several simultaneous processes and their products against each other, within the same sample. In a similar fashion, and also subject to qualification, the rate of formation of carbon-14 in various eras, the date of formation of organic matter within a certain period related to the isotope's half-life may be estimated, because the carbon-14 becomes trapped when the organic matter grows and incorporates the new carbon-14 from the air. Thereafter, the amount of carbon-14 in organic matter decreases according to decay processes that may also be independently cross-checked by other means (such as checking the carbon-14 in individual tree rings, for example).
</p>
<h3><span id="Szilard.E2.80.93Chalmers_effect"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Szilard–Chalmers_effect">Szilard–Chalmers effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Szilard–Chalmers effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Szilard–Chalmers effect is the breaking of a chemical bond as a result of a kinetic energy imparted from radioactive decay.  It operates by the absorption of neutrons by an atom and subsequent emission of <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, often with significant amounts of kinetic energy. This kinetic energy, by <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's third law</a>, pushes back on the decaying atom, which causes it to move with enough speed to break a chemical bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> This effect can be used to separate isotopes by chemical means.
</p><p>The Szilard–Chalmers effect was discovered in 1934 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3_Szil%C3%A1rd" title="Leó Szilárd">Leó Szilárd</a> and Thomas A. Chalmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> They observed that after bombardment by neutrons,  the breaking of a bond in liquid ethyl iodide allowed radioactive iodine to be removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_radioactive_nuclides">Origins of radioactive nuclides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Origins of radioactive nuclides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">Nucleosynthesis</a></div>
<p>Radioactive <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclides</a> found in the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> are residues from ancient <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova</a> explosions that occurred before the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. They are the fraction of radionuclides that survived from that time, through the formation of the primordial solar <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebula</a>, through planet <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>, and up to the present time. The naturally occurring short-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic" title="Radiogenic">radiogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a> found in today's <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a>, are the daughters of those radioactive <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclides</a>. Another minor source of naturally occurring radioactive nuclides are <a href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_nuclide" title="Cosmogenic nuclide">cosmogenic nuclides</a>, that are formed by cosmic ray bombardment of material in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> or <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>. The decay of the radionuclides in rocks of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> contribute significantly to <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_internal_heat_budget" title="Earth's internal heat budget">Earth's internal heat budget</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decay_chains_and_multiple_modes">Decay chains and multiple modes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Decay chains and multiple modes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_stability" title="Valley of stability">Valley of stability</a></div>
<p>The daughter nuclide of a decay event may also be unstable (radioactive). In this case, it too will decay, producing radiation. The resulting second daughter nuclide may also be radioactive. This can lead to a sequence of several decay events called a <i><a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a></i> (see this article for specific details of important natural decay chains). Eventually, a stable nuclide is produced. Any decay daughters that are the result of an alpha decay will also result in helium atoms being created.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg/290px-Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg/435px-Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg/580px-Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gammaspektrum_Uranerz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gamma_spectroscopy" title="Gamma spectroscopy">Gamma-ray energy spectrum</a> of uranium ore (inset). Gamma-rays are emitted by decaying <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclides</a>, and the gamma-ray energy can be used to characterize the decay (which nuclide is decaying to which). Here, using the gamma-ray spectrum, several nuclides that are typical of the decay chain of <sup>238</sup>U have been identified: <sup>226</sup>Ra, <sup>214</sup>Pb, <sup>214</sup>Bi.</div></div></div>
<p>An example is the natural decay chain of <sup>238</sup>U:
</p>
<ul><li>Uranium-238 decays, through alpha-emission, with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000446300000000000♠"></span>4.463(3)</span> billion years to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-234" title="Thorium-234">thorium-234</a></li>
<li>which decays, through beta-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001241070000000000♠"></span>24.107(24)</span> days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protactinium-234" title="Protactinium-234">protactinium-234m</a></li>
<li>which decays, through beta-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000115900000000000♠"></span>1.159(11)</span> minutes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Uranium-234" title="Uranium-234">uranium-234</a></li>
<li>which decays, through alpha-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002245500000000000♠"></span>245.5(6)</span> thousand years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-230" title="Thorium-230">thorium-230</a></li>
<li>which decays, through alpha-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001754000000000000♠"></span>75.4(3)</span> thousand years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-10"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radium-226" title="Radium-226">radium-226</a></li>
<li>which decays, through alpha-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000160000000000000♠"></span>1.600(7)</span> thousand years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-11"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Radon-222" title="Radon-222">radon-222</a></li>
<li>which decays, through alpha-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000382150000000000♠"></span>3.8215(2)</span> days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-12"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polonium-218" title="Polonium-218">polonium-218</a></li>
<li>which decays, through alpha-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000309700000000000♠"></span>3.097(12)</span> minutes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-13"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead-214" title="Lead-214">lead-214</a></li>
<li>which decays, through beta-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001270600000000000♠"></span>27.06(7)</span> minutes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-14"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bismuth-214" title="Bismuth-214">bismuth-214</a></li>
<li>which decays, through beta-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001199000000000000♠"></span>19.9(4)</span> minutes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-15"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polonium-214" title="Polonium-214">polonium-214</a></li>
<li>which decays, through alpha-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002163470000000000♠"></span>163.47(3)</span> microseconds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-16"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead-210" title="Lead-210">lead-210</a></li>
<li>which decays, through beta-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001222000000000000♠"></span>22.20(22)</span> years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-17"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bismuth-210" title="Bismuth-210">bismuth-210</a></li>
<li>which decays, through beta-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000501199999999999♠"></span>5.012(5)</span> days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-18"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Polonium-210" title="Polonium-210">polonium-210</a></li>
<li>which decays, through alpha-emission, with a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002138376000000000♠"></span>138.376(2)</span> days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-19"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead-206" title="Lead-206">lead-206</a>, which is a stable nuclide.</li></ul>
<p>Some radionuclides may have several different paths of decay. For example, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001359400000000000♠"></span>35.94(6)</span>%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-20"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bismuth-212" title="Bismuth-212">bismuth-212</a> decays, through alpha-emission, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thallium-208" title="Thallium-208">thallium-208</a> while <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001640600000000000♠"></span>64.06(6)</span>%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2020_32-21"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2020-32">[30]</a></sup> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bismuth-212" title="Bismuth-212">bismuth-212</a> decays, through beta-emission, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polonium-212" title="Polonium-212">polonium-212</a>. Both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thallium-208" title="Thallium-208">thallium-208</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polonium-212" title="Polonium-212">polonium-212</a> are radioactive daughter products of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bismuth-212" title="Bismuth-212">bismuth-212</a>, and both decay directly to stable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead-208" title="Lead-208">lead-208</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">

The difference between isotope and nuclide is explained at <a href="/wiki/Isotope#Isotope_vs._nuclide" title="Isotope">Isotope § Isotope vs. nuclide</a>.</span>
</li>

</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inline">Inline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Inline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General">General</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Radioactive_decay&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: General">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
